			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Hydrogen</h1>

									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">This article is about the chemistry of hydrogen. For the physics of atomic hydrogen, see <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">Hydrogen atom</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Hydrogen (disambiguation)">Hydrogen (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<td colspan="2" class="noprint" style="text-align:center;background:#e7ff8f">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_hydrogen" title="Template:Infobox hydrogen"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_hydrogen" title="Template talk:Infobox hydrogen"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_hydrogen&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
| <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a> | <a href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q556" class="extiw" title="d:Q556">in Wikidata</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Hydrogen</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with <a href="/wiki/Chemical_symbol" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical symbol">chemical symbol</a>&#160;<b>H</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>&#160;1. With a <a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">standard atomic weight</a> of circa <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7000100800000000000♠</span>1.008</span>, hydrogen is the lightest element on the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Monatomic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monatomic">monatomic</a> form (H) is the <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">most abundant</a> chemical substance in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a>, constituting roughly 75% of all <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryonic</a> mass.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[note 1]</a></sup> Non-<a href="/wiki/Stellar_remnant" class="mw-redirect" title="Stellar remnant">remnant</a> <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> are mainly composed of hydrogen in the <a href="/wiki/Plasma_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Plasma state">plasma state</a>. The most common <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of hydrogen, termed <i>protium</i> (name rarely used, symbol <sup>1</sup>H), has one <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a> and no <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>.</p>
<p>The universal emergence of atomic hydrogen first occurred during the <a href="/wiki/Recombination_(cosmology)" title="Recombination (cosmology)">recombination epoch</a>. At <a href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>, hydrogen is a <a href="/wiki/Transparency_(optics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Transparency (optics)">colorless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odorless" class="mw-redirect" title="Odorless">odorless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">tasteless</a>, non-toxic, <a href="/wiki/Nonmetal" title="Nonmetal">nonmetallic</a>, highly <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustible</a> <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecule" title="Diatomic molecule">diatomic</a> <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_formula" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular formula">molecular formula</a> H<sub>2</sub>. Since hydrogen readily forms <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent</a> compounds with most nonmetallic elements, most of the hydrogen on Earth exists in <a href="/wiki/Molecular_geometry" title="Molecular geometry">molecular forms</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> or <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>. Hydrogen plays a particularly important role in <a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_reaction" title="Acid–base reaction">acid–base reactions</a> because most acid-base reactions involve the exchange of protons between soluble molecules. In <a href="/wiki/Ionic_compound" title="Ionic compound">ionic compounds</a>, hydrogen can take the form of a negative charge (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Anion" class="mw-redirect" title="Anion">anion</a>) when it is known as a <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydride</a>, or as a positively charged (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Cation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cation">cation</a>) <a href="/wiki/Chemical_species" title="Chemical species">species</a> denoted by the symbol H<sup>+</sup>. The hydrogen cation is written as though composed of a bare proton, but in reality, hydrogen cations in ionic compounds are always more complex. As the only neutral atom for which the <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schrödinger equation">Schrödinger equation</a> can be solved analytically,<sup id="cite_ref-Laursen04_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Laursen04-12">[11]</a></sup> study of the energetics and bonding of the hydrogen atom has played a key role in the development of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>.</p>
<p>Hydrogen gas was first artificially produced in the early 16th century by the reaction of acids on metals. In 1766–81, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a> was the first to recognize that hydrogen gas was a discrete substance,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> and that it produces water when burned, the property for which it was later named: in Greek, hydrogen means "water-former".</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_production" title="Hydrogen production">Industrial production</a> is mainly from steam reforming natural gas, and less often from more energy-intensive methods such as the <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis of water</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> Most hydrogen is used near the site of its production, the two largest uses being <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> processing (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Hydrocracking" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrocracking">hydrocracking</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> production, mostly for the fertilizer market. Hydrogen is a concern in <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a> as it can <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_embrittlement" title="Hydrogen embrittlement">embrittle</a> many metals,<sup id="cite_ref-Rogers_1999_1057.E2.80.931064_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers_1999_1057.E2.80.931064-15">[14]</a></sup> complicating the design of pipelines and storage tanks.<sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combustion">Combustion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A black cup-like object hanging by its bottom with blue glow coming out of its opening." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg/170px-Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg" width="170" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg/255px-Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg/340px-Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1612" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shuttle_Main_Engine_Test_Firing_cropped_edited_and_reduced.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Main_Engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Space Shuttle Main Engine">Space Shuttle Main Engine</a> burnt hydrogen with oxygen, producing a nearly invisible flame at full thrust.</div>
</div>
</div>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:19._%D0%95%D0%BA%D1%81%D0%BF%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%81%D0%BC%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%B0_%D0%BE%D0%B4_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4_%D0%B8_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B4%D1%83%D1%85.webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Explosion of a hydrogen–air mixture.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hydrogen gas (dihydrogen or molecular hydrogen)<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> is highly flammable and will burn in air at a very wide range of concentrations between 4% and 75% by volume.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy_of_combustion" class="mw-redirect" title="Enthalpy of combustion">enthalpy of combustion</a> is −286&#160;kJ/mol:<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>2 H<sub>2</sub>(g) + O<sub>2</sub>(g) → 2 H<sub>2</sub>O(l) + 572&#160;kJ (286&#160;kJ/mol)<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[note 2]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Hydrogen gas forms explosive mixtures with air in concentrations from 4–74% and with chlorine at 5–95%. The explosive reactions may be triggered by spark, heat, or sunlight. The hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Autoignition_temperature" title="Autoignition temperature">autoignition temperature</a>, the temperature of spontaneous ignition in air, is 500&#160;°C (932&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> Pure <a href="/wiki/Oxyhydrogen" title="Oxyhydrogen">hydrogen-oxygen</a> flames emit <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light and with high oxygen mix are nearly invisible to the naked eye, as illustrated by the faint plume of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Main_Engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Space Shuttle Main Engine">Space Shuttle Main Engine</a>, compared to the highly visible plume of a <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Solid_Rocket_Booster" title="Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster">Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster</a>, which uses an <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_perchlorate_composite_propellant" title="Ammonium perchlorate composite propellant">ammonium perchlorate composite</a>. The detection of a burning hydrogen leak may require a <a href="/wiki/Flame_detector" title="Flame detector">flame detector</a>; such leaks can be very dangerous. Hydrogen flames in other conditions are blue, resembling blue natural gas flames.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hindenburg_disaster" title="Hindenburg disaster">destruction of the Hindenburg airship</a> was a notorious example of hydrogen combustion and the cause is still debated. The visible orange flames in that incident were the result of a rich mixture of hydrogen to oxygen combined with carbon compounds from the airship skin.</p>
<p>H<sub>2</sub> reacts with every oxidizing element. Hydrogen can react spontaneously and violently at room temperature with <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> to form the corresponding hydrogen halides, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a>, which are also potentially dangerous <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electron_energy_levels">Electron energy levels</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">Hydrogen atom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_atom.svg" class="image"><img alt="Drawing of a light-gray large sphere with a cut off quarter and a black small sphere and numbers 1.7x10−5 illustrating their relative diameters." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Hydrogen_atom.svg/220px-Hydrogen_atom.svg.png" width="220" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Hydrogen_atom.svg/330px-Hydrogen_atom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Hydrogen_atom.svg/440px-Hydrogen_atom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="270" data-file-height="246" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_atom.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Depiction of a hydrogen atom with size of central proton shown, and the atomic diameter shown as about twice the <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">Bohr model</a> radius (image not to scale)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ground_state" title="Ground state">ground state</a> <a href="/wiki/Energy_level" title="Energy level">energy level</a> of the electron in a hydrogen atom is −13.6&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> which is equivalent to an ultraviolet <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a> of roughly 91&#160;<a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">nm</a> wavelength.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The energy levels of hydrogen can be calculated fairly accurately using the <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">Bohr model</a> of the atom, which conceptualizes the electron as "orbiting" the proton in analogy to the Earth's orbit of the Sun. However, the atomic electron and proton are held together by <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Electromagnetic force">electromagnetic force</a>, while planets and celestial objects are held by <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. Because of the discretization of <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a> postulated in early <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> by Bohr, the electron in the Bohr model can only occupy certain allowed distances from the proton, and therefore only certain allowed energies.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>A more accurate description of the hydrogen atom comes from a purely quantum mechanical treatment that uses the <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schrödinger equation">Schrödinger equation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dirac_equation" title="Dirac equation">Dirac equation</a> or even the <a href="/wiki/Richard_Feynman" title="Richard Feynman">Feynman</a> <a href="/wiki/Path_integral_formulation" title="Path integral formulation">path integral formulation</a> to calculate the <a href="/wiki/Probability_amplitude" title="Probability amplitude">probability density</a> of the electron around the proton.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> The most complicated treatments allow for the small effects of <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_polarization" title="Vacuum polarization">vacuum polarization</a>. In the quantum mechanical treatment, the electron in a ground state hydrogen atom has no angular momentum at all—illustrating how the "planetary orbit" differs from electron motion.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elemental_molecular_forms">Elemental molecular forms</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Spin_isomers_of_hydrogen" title="Spin isomers of hydrogen">Spin isomers of hydrogen</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Liquid_hydrogen_bubblechamber.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Two bright circles on dark background, both contain numerous thin black lines inside." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Liquid_hydrogen_bubblechamber.jpg/170px-Liquid_hydrogen_bubblechamber.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Liquid_hydrogen_bubblechamber.jpg/255px-Liquid_hydrogen_bubblechamber.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Liquid_hydrogen_bubblechamber.jpg/340px-Liquid_hydrogen_bubblechamber.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="852" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Liquid_hydrogen_bubblechamber.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First tracks observed in <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Bubble_chamber" title="Bubble chamber">bubble chamber</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Bevatron" title="Bevatron">Bevatron</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There exist two different <a href="/wiki/Spin_isomers_of_hydrogen" title="Spin isomers of hydrogen">spin isomers of hydrogen</a> diatomic molecules that differ by the relative <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> of their nuclei.<sup id="cite_ref-uigi_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uigi-28">[26]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Orthohydrogen" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthohydrogen">orthohydrogen</a> form, the spins of the two protons are parallel and form a triplet state with a molecular spin quantum number of 1 (<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>+<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>); in the <a href="/wiki/Parahydrogen" class="mw-redirect" title="Parahydrogen">parahydrogen</a> form the spins are antiparallel and form a singlet with a molecular spin quantum number of 0 (<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>–<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>). At standard temperature and pressure, hydrogen gas contains about 25% of the para form and 75% of the ortho form, also known as the "normal form".<sup id="cite_ref-Tikhonov_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tikhonov-29">[27]</a></sup> The equilibrium ratio of orthohydrogen to parahydrogen depends on temperature, but because the ortho form is an <a href="/wiki/Excited_state" title="Excited state">excited state</a> and has a higher energy than the para form, it is unstable and cannot be purified. At very low temperatures, the equilibrium state is composed almost exclusively of the para form. The liquid and gas phase thermal properties of pure parahydrogen differ significantly from those of the normal form because of differences in rotational heat capacities, as discussed more fully in <i><a href="/wiki/Spin_isomers_of_hydrogen" title="Spin isomers of hydrogen">spin isomers of hydrogen</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-30">[28]</a></sup> The ortho/para distinction also occurs in other hydrogen-containing molecules or functional groups, such as water and <a href="/wiki/Methylene_group" title="Methylene group">methylene</a>, but is of little significance for their thermal properties.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The uncatalyzed interconversion between para and ortho H<sub>2</sub> increases with increasing temperature; thus rapidly condensed H<sub>2</sub> contains large quantities of the high-energy ortho form that converts to the para form very slowly.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> The ortho/para ratio in condensed H<sub>2</sub> is an important consideration in the preparation and storage of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a>: the conversion from ortho to para is <a href="/wiki/Exothermic" class="mw-redirect" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a> and produces enough heat to evaporate some of the hydrogen liquid, leading to loss of liquefied material. <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">Catalysts</a> for the ortho-para interconversion, such as <a href="/wiki/Ferric_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferric oxide">ferric oxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated carbon</a>, platinized asbestos, rare earth metals, uranium compounds, <a href="/wiki/Chromic_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Chromic oxide">chromic oxide</a>, or some nickel<sup id="cite_ref-Amos98_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amos98-33">[31]</a></sup> compounds, are used during hydrogen cooling.<sup id="cite_ref-Svadlenak_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svadlenak-34">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phases">Phases</span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">Gaseous</a> hydrogen</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">Liquid hydrogen</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Slush_hydrogen" title="Slush hydrogen">Slush hydrogen</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Solid_hydrogen" title="Solid hydrogen">Solid hydrogen</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Metallic_hydrogen" title="Metallic hydrogen">Metallic hydrogen</a></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Category:Hydrogen_compounds" title="Category:Hydrogen compounds">Category:Hydrogen compounds</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Covalent_and_organic_compounds">Covalent and organic compounds</span></h4>
<p>While H<sub>2</sub> is not very reactive under standard conditions, it does form compounds with most elements. Hydrogen can form compounds with elements that are more <a href="/wiki/Electronegative" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronegative">electronegative</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a> (e.g., F, Cl, Br, I), or <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>; in these compounds hydrogen takes on a partial positive charge.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> When bonded to <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, hydrogen can participate in a form of medium-strength noncovalent bonding with the hydrogen of other similar molecules, a phenomenon called <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonding</a> that is critical to the stability of many biological molecules.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> Hydrogen also forms compounds with less electronegative elements, such as <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloids</a>, where it takes on a partial negative charge. These compounds are often known as <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydrides</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hydrogen forms a vast array of compounds with <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, and an even vaster array with <a href="/wiki/Heteroatoms" class="mw-redirect" title="Heteroatoms">heteroatoms</a> that, because of their general association with living things, are called <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hydrocarbon_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hydrocarbon-39">[37]</a></sup> The study of their properties is known as <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> and their study in the context of living <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> is known as <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> By some definitions, "organic" compounds are only required to contain carbon. However, most of them also contain hydrogen, and because it is the carbon-hydrogen bond which gives this class of compounds most of its particular chemical characteristics, carbon-hydrogen bonds are required in some definitions of the word "organic" in chemistry.<sup id="cite_ref-hydrocarbon_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hydrocarbon-39">[37]</a></sup> Millions of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> are known, and they are usually formed by complicated synthetic pathways that seldom involve elementary hydrogen.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrides">Hydrides</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">Hydride</a></div>
<p>Compounds of hydrogen are often called <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydrides</a>, a term that is used fairly loosely. The term "hydride" suggests that the H atom has acquired a negative or anionic character, denoted H<sup>−</sup>, and is used when hydrogen forms a compound with a more <a href="/wiki/Electropositive" class="mw-redirect" title="Electropositive">electropositive</a> element. The existence of the <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydride anion</a>, suggested by <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_N._Lewis" title="Gilbert N. Lewis">Gilbert N. Lewis</a> in 1916 for group 1 and 2 salt-like hydrides, was demonstrated by Moers in 1920 by the electrolysis of molten <a href="/wiki/Lithium_hydride" title="Lithium hydride">lithium hydride</a> (LiH), producing a <a href="/wiki/Stoichiometric" class="mw-redirect" title="Stoichiometric">stoichiometry</a> quantity of hydrogen at the anode.<sup id="cite_ref-Moers_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moers-42">[40]</a></sup> For hydrides other than group 1 and 2 metals, the term is quite misleading, considering the low electronegativity of hydrogen. An exception in group 2 hydrides is <span class="chemf nowrap">BeH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>, which is polymeric. In <a href="/wiki/Lithium_aluminium_hydride" title="Lithium aluminium hydride">lithium aluminium hydride</a>, the <span class="chemf nowrap">AlH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">−<br />
4</span></span> anion carries hydridic centers firmly attached to the Al(III).</p>
<p>Although hydrides can be formed with almost all main-group elements, the number and combination of possible compounds varies widely; for example, more than 100 binary borane hydrides are known, but only one binary aluminium hydride.<sup id="cite_ref-Downs_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Downs-43">[41]</a></sup> Binary <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a> hydride has not yet been identified, although larger complexes exist.<sup id="cite_ref-Hibbs_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hibbs-44">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_chemistry" title="Inorganic chemistry">inorganic chemistry</a>, hydrides can also serve as <a href="/wiki/Bridging_ligand" title="Bridging ligand">bridging ligands</a> that link two metal centers in a <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complex</a>. This function is particularly common in <a href="/wiki/Group_13_element" class="mw-redirect" title="Group 13 element">group 13 elements</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Borane" title="Borane">boranes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> hydrides) and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> complexes, as well as in clustered <a href="/wiki/Carborane" title="Carborane">carboranes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Miessler_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miessler-45">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Protons_and_acids">Protons and acids</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_reaction" title="Acid–base reaction">Acid–base reaction</a></div>
<p>Oxidation of hydrogen removes its electron and gives <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_ion" title="Hydrogen ion">H<sup>+</sup></a>, which contains no electrons and a <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> which is usually composed of one proton. That is why <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br /></span></span> is often called a proton. This species is central to discussion of <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>. Under the <a href="/wiki/Bronsted-Lowry_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronsted-Lowry theory">Bronsted-Lowry theory</a>, acids are proton donors, while bases are proton acceptors.</p>
<p>A bare proton, <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br /></span></span>, cannot exist in solution or in ionic crystals because of its unstoppable attraction to other atoms or molecules with electrons. Except at the high temperatures associated with plasmas, such protons cannot be removed from the <a href="/wiki/Electron_cloud" class="mw-redirect" title="Electron cloud">electron clouds</a> of atoms and molecules, and will remain attached to them. However, the term 'proton' is sometimes used loosely and metaphorically to refer to positively charged or <a href="/wiki/Cation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cation">cationic</a> hydrogen attached to other species in this fashion, and as such is denoted "<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br /></span></span>" without any implication that any single protons exist freely as a species.</p>
<p>To avoid the implication of the naked "solvated proton" in solution, acidic aqueous solutions are sometimes considered to contain a less unlikely fictitious species, termed the "<a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">hydronium</a> ion" (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br /></span></span>). However, even in this case, such solvated hydrogen cations are more realistically conceived as being organized into clusters that form species closer to H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
9</span><span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br />
4</span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Okumura_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Okumura-46">[44]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Oxonium_ion" title="Oxonium ion">oxonium ions</a> are found when water is in acidic solution with other solvents.<sup id="cite_ref-Perdoncin_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perdoncin-47">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although exotic on Earth, one of the most common ions in the universe is the <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br />
3</span></span> ion, known as <a href="/wiki/Trihydrogen_cation" title="Trihydrogen cation">protonated molecular hydrogen</a> or the trihydrogen cation.<sup id="cite_ref-Carrington_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrington-48">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_hydrogen" title="Isotopes of hydrogen">Isotopes of hydrogen</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg/220px-Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg" width="220" height="73" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg/330px-Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg/440px-Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_discharge_tube.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hydrogen discharge (spectrum) tube</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg/220px-Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg" width="220" height="73" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg/330px-Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg/440px-Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Deuterium discharge (spectrum) tube</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Protium.svg" class="image"><img alt="Schematic drawing of a positive atom in the center orbited by a negative particle." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Protium.svg/170px-Protium.svg.png" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Protium.svg/255px-Protium.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Protium.svg/340px-Protium.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="64" data-file-height="64" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Protium.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Protium, the most common <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of hydrogen, has one proton and one electron. Unique among all stable isotopes, it has no neutrons (see <a href="/wiki/Diproton" class="mw-redirect" title="Diproton">diproton</a> for a discussion of why others do not exist).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hydrogen has three naturally occurring isotopes, denoted <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">1<br /></span>H</span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">2<br /></span>H</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">3<br /></span>H</span>. Other, highly unstable nuclei (<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">4<br /></span>H</span> to <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">7<br /></span>H</span>) have been synthesized in the laboratory but not observed in nature.<sup id="cite_ref-Gurov_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gurov-49">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Korsheninnikov_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korsheninnikov-50">[48]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">1<br /></span>H</span></b> is the most common hydrogen isotope with an abundance of more than 99.98%. Because the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> of this isotope consists of only a single proton, it is given the descriptive but rarely used formal name <i>protium</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">2<br /></span>H</span></b>, the other stable hydrogen isotope, is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a></i> and contains one proton and one <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> in the nucleus. All deuterium in the universe is thought to have been produced at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, and has endured since that time. Deuterium is not radioactive, and does not represent a significant toxicity hazard. Water enriched in molecules that include deuterium instead of normal hydrogen is called <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a>. Deuterium and its compounds are used as a non-radioactive label in chemical experiments and in solvents for <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">1<br /></span>H</span>-<a href="/wiki/NMR_spectroscopy" class="mw-redirect" title="NMR spectroscopy">NMR spectroscopy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> Heavy water is used as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">neutron moderator</a> and coolant for nuclear reactors. Deuterium is also a potential fuel for commercial <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">3<br /></span>H</span></b> is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a></i> and contains one proton and two neutrons in its nucleus. It is radioactive, decaying into <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">helium-3</a> through <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 12.32 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Miessler_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miessler-45">[43]</a></sup> It is so radioactive that it can be used in <a href="/wiki/Radioluminescent_paint" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioluminescent paint">luminous paint</a>, making it useful in such things as watches. The glass prevents the small amount of radiation from getting out.<sup id="cite_ref-Traub95_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Traub95-54">[52]</a></sup> Small amounts of tritium are produced naturally by the interaction of cosmic rays with atmospheric gases; tritium has also been released during <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_testing" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear testing">nuclear weapons tests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup> It is used in nuclear fusion reactions,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup> as a tracer in <a href="/wiki/Isotope_geochemistry" title="Isotope geochemistry">isotope geochemistry</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup> and in specialized <a href="/wiki/Self-powered_lighting" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-powered lighting">self-powered lighting</a> devices.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> Tritium has also been used in chemical and biological labeling experiments as a <a href="/wiki/Radiolabel" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiolabel">radiolabel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-holte_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holte-59">[57]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Hydrogen is the only element that has different names for its isotopes in common use today. During the early study of radioactivity, various heavy radioactive isotopes were given their own names, but such names are no longer used, except for deuterium and tritium. The symbols D and T (instead of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">2<br /></span>H</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">3<br /></span>H</span>) are sometimes used for deuterium and tritium, but the corresponding symbol for protium, P, is already in use for <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> and thus is not available for protium.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> In its <a href="/wiki/IUPAC_nomenclature" class="mw-redirect" title="IUPAC nomenclature">nomenclatural</a> guidelines, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> allows any of D, T, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">2<br /></span>H</span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">3<br /></span>H</span> to be used, although <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">2<br /></span>H</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">3<br /></span>H</span> are preferred.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Exotic_atom" title="Exotic atom">exotic atom</a> <a href="/wiki/Muonium" title="Muonium">muonium</a> (symbol Mu), composed of an <a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">antimuon</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>, is also sometimes considered as a light radioisotope of hydrogen, due to the mass difference between the antimuon and the electron.<sup id="cite_ref-Gold_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gold-62">[60]</a></sup> which was discovered in 1960<sup id="cite_ref-Hughes_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes-63">[61]</a></sup> During the muon's <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7000220000000000000♠</span>2.2&#160;<a href="/wiki/Microsecond" title="Microsecond">µs</a></span> lifetime, muonium can enter into compounds such as muonium chloride (MuCl) or sodium muonide (NaMu), analogous to <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydride" title="Sodium hydride">sodium hydride</a> respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-iupac_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iupac-64">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_use">Discovery and use</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_hydrogen_technologies" title="Timeline of hydrogen technologies">Timeline of hydrogen technologies</a></div>
<p>In 1671, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> discovered and described the reaction between <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> filings and dilute <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>, which results in the production of hydrogen gas.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> In 1766, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a> was the first to recognize hydrogen gas as a discrete substance, by naming the gas from a <a href="/wiki/Metal-acid_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Metal-acid reaction">metal-acid reaction</a> "inflammable air". He speculated that "inflammable air" was in fact identical to the hypothetical substance called "<a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cav766_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cav766-68">[66]</a></sup> and further finding in 1781 that the gas produces water when burned. He is usually given credit for the discovery of hydrogen as an element.<sup id="cite_ref-Nostrand_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nostrand-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nbb_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-8">[8]</a></sup> In 1783, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> gave the element the name hydrogen (from the Greek ὑδρο- <i>hydro</i> meaning "water" and -γενής <i>genes</i> meaning "creator")<sup id="cite_ref-Stwertka_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka-9">[9]</a></sup> when he and <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace" title="Pierre-Simon Laplace">Laplace</a> reproduced Cavendish's finding that water is produced when hydrogen is burned.<sup id="cite_ref-nbb_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_(by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre)RENEW.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_%28by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre%29RENEW.jpg/180px-Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_%28by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre%29RENEW.jpg" width="180" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_%28by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre%29RENEW.jpg/270px-Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_%28by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre%29RENEW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_%28by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre%29RENEW.jpg/360px-Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_%28by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre%29RENEW.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1584" data-file-height="2158" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier_(by_Louis_Jean_Desire_Delaistre)RENEW.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Antoine-Laurent de Lavoisier</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lavoisier produced hydrogen for his experiments on mass conservation by reacting a flux of steam with metallic <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> through an incandescent iron tube heated in a fire. Anaerobic oxidation of iron by the protons of water at high temperature can be schematically represented by the set of following reactions:</p>
<dl>
<dd>&#160;&#160; Fe + &#160;&#160; H<sub>2</sub>O → FeO + H<sub>2</sub></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>2 Fe + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O → Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 3 H<sub>2</sub></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>3 Fe + 4 H<sub>2</sub>O → Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + 4 H<sub>2</sub></dd>
</dl>
<p>Many metals such as <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a> undergo a similar reaction with water leading to the production of hydrogen.</p>
<p>Hydrogen was <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquefied</a> for the first time by <a href="/wiki/James_Dewar" title="James Dewar">James Dewar</a> in 1898 by using <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_cooling" title="Regenerative cooling">regenerative cooling</a> and his invention, the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_flask" title="Vacuum flask">vacuum flask</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nbb_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-8">[8]</a></sup> He produced <a href="/wiki/Solid_hydrogen" title="Solid hydrogen">solid hydrogen</a> the next year.<sup id="cite_ref-nbb_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">Deuterium</a> was discovered in December 1931 by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Urey" title="Harold Urey">Harold Urey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> was prepared in 1934 by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Oliphant" title="Mark Oliphant">Mark Oliphant</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Harteck" title="Paul Harteck">Paul Harteck</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nostrand_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nostrand-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">Heavy water</a>, which consists of deuterium in the place of regular hydrogen, was discovered by Urey's group in 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-nbb_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Isaac_de_Rivaz" title="François Isaac de Rivaz">François Isaac de Rivaz</a> built the first <a href="/wiki/De_Rivaz_engine" title="De Rivaz engine">de Rivaz engine</a>, an internal combustion engine powered by a mixture of hydrogen and oxygen in 1806. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Daniel_Clarke" title="Edward Daniel Clarke">Edward Daniel Clarke</a> invented the hydrogen gas blowpipe in 1819. The <a href="/wiki/D%C3%B6bereiner%27s_lamp" title="Döbereiner's lamp">Döbereiner's lamp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limelight" title="Limelight">limelight</a> were invented in 1823.<sup id="cite_ref-nbb_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first hydrogen-filled <a href="/wiki/Balloon" title="Balloon">balloon</a> was invented by <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Charles" title="Jacques Charles">Jacques Charles</a> in 1783.<sup id="cite_ref-nbb_8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-8">[8]</a></sup> Hydrogen provided the lift for the first reliable form of air-travel following the 1852 invention of the first hydrogen-lifted airship by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Giffard" title="Henri Giffard">Henri Giffard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nbb_8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-8">[8]</a></sup> German count <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_von_Zeppelin" title="Ferdinand von Zeppelin">Ferdinand von Zeppelin</a> promoted the idea of rigid airships lifted by hydrogen that later were called <a href="/wiki/Zeppelin" title="Zeppelin">Zeppelins</a>; the first of which had its maiden flight in 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-nbb_8-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-8">[8]</a></sup> Regularly scheduled flights started in 1910 and by the outbreak of World War I in August 1914, they had carried 35,000 passengers without a serious incident. Hydrogen-lifted airships were used as observation platforms and bombers during the war.</p>
<p>The first non-stop transatlantic crossing was made by the British airship <i><a href="/wiki/R34_(airship)" class="mw-redirect" title="R34 (airship)">R34</a></i> in 1919. Regular passenger service resumed in the 1920s and the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> reserves in the United States promised increased safety, but the U.S. government refused to sell the gas for this purpose. Therefore, H<sub>2</sub> was used in the <a href="/wiki/LZ_129_Hindenburg" title="LZ 129 Hindenburg"><i>Hindenburg</i></a> airship, which was destroyed in a midair fire over <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> on 6 May 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-nbb_8-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-8">[8]</a></sup> The incident was broadcast live on radio and filmed. Ignition of leaking hydrogen is widely assumed to be the cause, but later investigations pointed to the ignition of the <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminized</a> fabric coating by <a href="/wiki/Static_electricity" title="Static electricity">static electricity</a>. But the damage to hydrogen's reputation as a <a href="/wiki/Lifting_gas" title="Lifting gas">lifting gas</a> was already done.</p>
<p>In the same year the first <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen-cooled_turbogenerator" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen-cooled turbogenerator">hydrogen-cooled turbogenerator</a> went into service with gaseous hydrogen as a <a href="/wiki/Coolant" title="Coolant">coolant</a> in the rotor and the stator in 1937 at <a href="/wiki/Dayton,_Ohio" title="Dayton, Ohio">Dayton</a>, Ohio, by the Dayton Power &amp; Light Co.;<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup> because of the thermal conductivity of hydrogen gas, this is the most common type in its field today.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nickel_hydrogen_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel hydrogen battery">nickel hydrogen battery</a> was used for the first time in 1977 aboard the U.S. Navy's Navigation technology satellite-2 (NTS-2).<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup> For example, the <a href="/wiki/ISS" class="mw-redirect" title="ISS">ISS</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/2001_Mars_Odyssey" title="2001 Mars Odyssey">Mars Odyssey</a><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> are equipped with nickel-hydrogen batteries. In the dark part of its orbit, the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> is also powered by nickel-hydrogen batteries, which were finally replaced in May 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> more than 19 years after launch and 13 years beyond their design life.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_quantum_theory">Role in quantum theory</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Emission_spectrum-H.svg" class="image"><img alt="A line spectrum showing black background with narrow lines superimposed on it: one violet, one blue, one cyan, and one red." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Emission_spectrum-H.svg/500px-Emission_spectrum-H.svg.png" width="500" height="66" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Emission_spectrum-H.svg/750px-Emission_spectrum-H.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Emission_spectrum-H.svg/1000px-Emission_spectrum-H.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="757" data-file-height="100" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Emission_spectrum-H.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hydrogen emission spectrum lines in the visible range. These are the four visible lines of the <a href="/wiki/Balmer_series" title="Balmer series">Balmer series</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because of its simple atomic structure, consisting only of a proton and an electron, the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a>, together with the spectrum of light produced from it or absorbed by it, has been central to the development of the theory of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atomic</a> structure.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> Furthermore, study of the corresponding simplicity of the hydrogen molecule and the corresponding cation <a href="/wiki/H2%2B" class="mw-redirect" title="H2+"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br />
2</span></span></a> brought understanding of the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bond</a>, which followed shortly after the quantum mechanical treatment of the hydrogen atom had been developed in the mid-1920s.</p>
<p>One of the first quantum effects to be explicitly noticed (but not understood at the time) was a Maxwell observation involving hydrogen, half a century before full <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical theory</a> arrived. Maxwell observed that the <a href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" class="mw-redirect" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat capacity</a> of H<sub>2</sub> unaccountably departs from that of a <a href="/wiki/Diatomic" class="mw-redirect" title="Diatomic">diatomic</a> gas below room temperature and begins to increasingly resemble that of a monatomic gas at cryogenic temperatures. According to quantum theory, this behavior arises from the spacing of the (quantized) rotational energy levels, which are particularly wide-spaced in H<sub>2</sub> because of its low mass. These widely spaced levels inhibit equal partition of heat energy into rotational motion in hydrogen at low temperatures. Diatomic gases composed of heavier atoms do not have such widely spaced levels and do not exhibit the same effect.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berman-77">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Antihydrogen" title="Antihydrogen">Antihydrogen</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><br /></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">H</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br /></span></span>) is the <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a> counterpart to hydrogen. It consists of an <a href="/wiki/Antiproton" title="Antiproton">antiproton</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a>. Antihydrogen is the only type of antimatter atom to have been produced as of 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-char15_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-char15-78">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Keller15_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keller15-79">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_occurrence">Natural occurrence</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A white-green cotton-like clog on black background." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg/220px-Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg" width="220" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg/330px-Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg/440px-Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1127" data-file-height="1201" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nursery_of_New_Stars_-_GPN-2000-000972.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/NGC_604" title="NGC 604">NGC 604</a>, a giant <a href="/wiki/H_II_region" title="H II region">region of ionized hydrogen</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Triangulum_Galaxy" title="Triangulum Galaxy">Triangulum Galaxy</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hydrogen, as atomic H, is the most <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">abundant</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> in the universe, making up 75% of <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">normal matter</a> by <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> and more than 90% by number of atoms. (Most of the mass of the universe, however, is not in the form of chemical-element type matter, but rather is postulated to occur as yet-undetected forms of mass such as <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup>) This element is found in great abundance in stars and <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giant</a> planets. <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">Molecular clouds</a> of H<sub>2</sub> are associated with <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">star formation</a>. Hydrogen plays a vital role in powering <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Proton-proton_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Proton-proton reaction">proton-proton reaction</a> and the <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">CNO cycle</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout the universe, hydrogen is mostly found in the <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atomic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> states, with properties quite different from those of molecular hydrogen. As a plasma, hydrogen's electron and proton are not bound together, resulting in very high electrical conductivity and high emissivity (producing the light from the Sun and other stars). The charged particles are highly influenced by magnetic and electric fields. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> they interact with the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> giving rise to <a href="/wiki/Birkeland_current" title="Birkeland current">Birkeland currents</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aurora_(phenomenon)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aurora (phenomenon)">aurora</a>. Hydrogen is found in the neutral atomic state in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>. The large amount of neutral hydrogen found in the damped Lyman-alpha systems is thought to dominate the cosmological baryonic density of the Universe up to <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> <i>z</i>=4.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under ordinary conditions on Earth, elemental hydrogen exists as the diatomic gas, H<sub>2</sub>. However, hydrogen gas is very rare in the Earth's atmosphere (1 <a href="/wiki/Part_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Part per million">ppm</a> by volume) because of its light weight, which enables it to <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_escape" title="Atmospheric escape">escape from Earth's gravity</a> more easily than heavier gases. However, hydrogen is the third most abundant element on the Earth's surface,<sup id="cite_ref-ArgonneBasic_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ArgonneBasic-83">[81]</a></sup> mostly in the form of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compounds</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> and water.<sup id="cite_ref-Miessler_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miessler-45">[43]</a></sup> Hydrogen gas is produced by some bacteria and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and is a natural component of <a href="/wiki/Flatus" class="mw-redirect" title="Flatus">flatus</a>, as is <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, itself a hydrogen source of increasing importance.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>A molecular form called <a href="/wiki/Protonated_molecular_hydrogen" class="mw-redirect" title="Protonated molecular hydrogen">protonated molecular hydrogen</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br />
3</span></span>) is found in the interstellar medium, where it is generated by ionization of molecular hydrogen from <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>. This charged ion has also been observed in the upper atmosphere of the planet <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. The ion is relatively stable in the environment of outer space due to the low temperature and density. <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br />
3</span></span> is one of the most abundant ions in the Universe, and it plays a notable role in the chemistry of the interstellar medium.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> Neutral <a href="/wiki/Triatomic_hydrogen" title="Triatomic hydrogen">triatomic hydrogen</a> H<sub>3</sub> can exist only in an excited form and is unstable.<sup id="cite_ref-couple_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-couple-86">[84]</a></sup> By contrast, the positive <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_molecular_ion" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen molecular ion">hydrogen molecular ion</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br />
2</span></span>) is a rare molecule in the universe.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_production" title="Hydrogen production">Hydrogen production</a></div>
<p><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> is produced in chemistry and biology laboratories, often as a by-product of other reactions; in industry for the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saturation_(chemistry)" title="Saturation (chemistry)">unsaturated</a> substrates; and in nature as a means of expelling <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reducing</a> equivalents in biochemical reactions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steam_reforming">Steam reforming</span></h3>
<p>Hydrogen can be prepared in several different ways, but economically the most important processes involve removal of hydrogen from hydrocarbons, as about 95% of hydrogen production came from steam reforming around year 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-Ogden_1999_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogden_1999-87">[85]</a></sup> Commercial bulk hydrogen is usually produced by the <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reforming</a> of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Oxtoby_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxtoby-88">[86]</a></sup> At high temperatures (1000–1400&#160;K, 700–1100&#160;°C or 1300–2000&#160;°F), steam (water vapor) reacts with <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> to yield <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> and <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>.</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span> → CO + 3 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>This reaction is favored at low pressures but is nonetheless conducted at high pressures (2.0 &#160;MPa, 20&#160;atm or 600&#160;<a href="/wiki/InHg" class="mw-redirect" title="InHg">inHg</a>). This is because high-pressure <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> is the most marketable product and <a href="/wiki/Pressure_swing_adsorption" title="Pressure swing adsorption">pressure swing adsorption</a> (PSA) purification systems work better at higher pressures. The product mixture is known as "<a href="/wiki/Synthesis_gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Synthesis gas">synthesis gas</a>" because it is often used directly for the production of <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> and related compounds. <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">Hydrocarbons</a> other than methane can be used to produce synthesis gas with varying product ratios. One of the many complications to this highly optimized technology is the formation of coke or carbon:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span> → C + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Consequently, steam reforming typically employs an excess of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span>. Additional hydrogen can be recovered from the steam by use of carbon monoxide through the <a href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Water gas shift reaction">water gas shift reaction</a>, especially with an <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a> catalyst. This reaction is also a common industrial source of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-Oxtoby_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxtoby-88">[86]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>CO + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Other important methods for <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> production include partial oxidation of hydrocarbons:<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> → 2 CO + 4 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>and the coal reaction, which can serve as a prelude to the shift reaction above:<sup id="cite_ref-Oxtoby_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxtoby-88">[86]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>C + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span> → CO + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Hydrogen is sometimes produced and consumed in the same industrial process, without being separated. In the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber process</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">production of ammonia</a>, hydrogen is generated from natural gas.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">Electrolysis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> to yield <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> also produces hydrogen as a co-product.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal-acid">Metal-acid</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Laboratory" title="Laboratory">laboratory</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> is usually prepared by the reaction of dilute <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_acid" title="Oxidizing acid">non-oxidizing acids</a> on some reactive metals such as <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> with <a href="/wiki/Kipp%27s_apparatus" title="Kipp's apparatus">Kipp's apparatus</a>.</p>
<dl>
<dd>Zn + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br /></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">2+<br /></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">Aluminium</a> can also produce <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> upon treatment with bases:</p>
<dl>
<dd>2 Al + 6 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">−<br /></span></span> → 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">Al(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">−<br />
4</span></span> + 3 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis of water</a> is a simple method of producing hydrogen. A low voltage current is run through the water, and gaseous oxygen forms at the <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a> while gaseous hydrogen forms at the <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathode</a>. Typically the cathode is made from platinum or another inert metal when producing hydrogen for storage. If, however, the gas is to be burnt on site, oxygen is desirable to assist the combustion, and so both electrodes would be made from inert metals. (Iron, for instance, would oxidize, and thus decrease the amount of oxygen given off.) The theoretical maximum efficiency (electricity used vs. energetic value of hydrogen produced) is in the range 80–94%.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span>(l) → 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>(g) + <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>(g)</dd>
</dl>
<p>An alloy of aluminium and <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a> in pellet form added to water can be used to generate hydrogen. The process also produces <a href="/wiki/Alumina" class="mw-redirect" title="Alumina">alumina</a>, but the expensive gallium, which prevents the formation of an oxide skin on the pellets, can be re-used. This has important potential implications for a hydrogen economy, as hydrogen can be produced on-site and does not need to be transported.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermochemical">Thermochemical</span></h3>
<p>There are more than 200 thermochemical cycles which can be used for <a href="/wiki/Water_splitting" title="Water splitting">water splitting</a>, around a dozen of these cycles such as the <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide_cycle" title="Iron oxide cycle">iron oxide cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cerium(IV)_oxide%E2%80%93cerium(III)_oxide_cycle" title="Cerium(IV) oxide–cerium(III) oxide cycle">cerium(IV) oxide–cerium(III) oxide cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc_zinc-oxide_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Zinc zinc-oxide cycle">zinc zinc-oxide cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur-iodine_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Sulfur-iodine cycle">sulfur-iodine cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper-chlorine_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Copper-chlorine cycle">copper-chlorine cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_sulfur_cycle" title="Hybrid sulfur cycle">hybrid sulfur cycle</a> are under research and in testing phase to produce hydrogen and oxygen from water and heat without using electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> A number of laboratories (including in France, Germany, Greece, Japan, and the USA) are developing thermochemical methods to produce hydrogen from solar energy and water.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anaerobic_corrosion">Anaerobic corrosion</span></h3>
