<p>Under anaerobic conditions, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steel_alloy" class="mw-redirect" title="Steel alloy">steel alloys</a> are slowly oxidized by the protons of water concomitantly reduced in molecular hydrogen (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>). The <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_corrosion" title="Anaerobic corrosion">anaerobic corrosion</a> of iron leads first to the formation of <a href="/wiki/Ferrous_hydroxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferrous hydroxide">ferrous hydroxide</a> (green rust) and can be described by the following reaction:</p>
<dl>
<dd>Fe + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>O → <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>In its turn, under anaerobic conditions, the <a href="/wiki/Ferrous_hydroxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferrous hydroxide">ferrous hydroxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> ) can be oxidized by the protons of water to form <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> and molecular hydrogen. This process is described by the <a href="/wiki/Schikorr_reaction" title="Schikorr reaction">Schikorr reaction</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>3 <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>O + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></dd>
<dd><i>ferrous hydroxide → magnetite + water + hydrogen</i></dd>
</dl>
<p>The well crystallized magnetite (<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span>) is thermodynamically more stable than the ferrous hydroxide (<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> ).</p>
<p>This process occurs during the anaerobic corrosion of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Anoxic_waters" title="Anoxic waters">oxygen-free</a> <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> and in reducing <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soils</a> below the <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_occurrence:_the_serpentinization_reaction">Geological occurrence: the serpentinization reaction</span></h3>
<p>In the absence of atmospheric oxygen (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>), in deep geological conditions prevailing far away from Earth atmosphere, hydrogen (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>) is produced during the process of <a href="/wiki/Serpentinization#Hydrogen_production_by_anaerobic_oxidation_of_fayalite_ferrous_ions" class="mw-redirect" title="Serpentinization">serpentinization</a> by the anaerobic oxidation by the water protons (H<sup>+</sup>) of the ferrous (Fe<sup>2+</sup>) silicate present in the crystal lattice of the <a href="/wiki/Fayalite" title="Fayalite">fayalite</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> iron-endmember). The corresponding reaction leading to the formation of <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a> (Si<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>) and hydrogen (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>) is the following:</p>
<dl>
<dd>3<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>O → 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span> + 3 Si<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> + 3 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></dd>
<dd><i>fayalite + water → magnetite + quartz + hydrogen</i></dd>
</dl>
<p>This reaction closely resembles the <a href="/wiki/Schikorr_reaction" title="Schikorr reaction">Schikorr reaction</a> observed in the anaerobic oxidation of the <a href="/wiki/Ferrous_hydroxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferrous hydroxide">ferrous hydroxide</a> in contact with water.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_in_transformers">Formation in transformers</span></h3>
<p>From all the fault gases formed in power <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">transformers</a>, hydrogen is the most common and is generated under most fault conditions; thus, formation of hydrogen is an early indication of serious problems in the transformer's life cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumption_in_processes">Consumption in processes</span></h3>
<p>Large quantities of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> are needed in the petroleum and chemical industries. The largest application of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> is for the processing ("upgrading") of fossil fuels, and in the production of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>. The key consumers of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> in the petrochemical plant include <a href="/wiki/Hydrodealkylation" title="Hydrodealkylation">hydrodealkylation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrodesulfurization" title="Hydrodesulfurization">hydrodesulfurization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrocracking" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrocracking">hydrocracking</a>. <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> has several other important uses. <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> is used as a hydrogenating agent, particularly in increasing the level of saturation of unsaturated fats and <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">oils</a> (found in items such as margarine), and in the production of <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>. It is similarly the source of hydrogen in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>. <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a> of metallic <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hydrogen is highly soluble in many <a href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" title="Rare earth element">rare earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a><sup id="cite_ref-Takeshita_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Takeshita-98">[96]</a></sup> and is soluble in both nanocrystalline and <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_metal" title="Amorphous metal">amorphous metals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kirchheim1_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirchheim1-99">[97]</a></sup> Hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">solubility</a> in metals is influenced by local distortions or impurities in the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_lattice" class="mw-redirect" title="Crystal lattice">crystal lattice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kirchheim2_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirchheim2-100">[98]</a></sup> These properties may be useful when hydrogen is purified by passage through hot <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a> disks, but the gas's high solubility is a metallurgical problem, contributing to the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_embrittlement" title="Hydrogen embrittlement">embrittlement</a> of many metals,<sup id="cite_ref-Rogers_1999_1057.E2.80.931064_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers_1999_1057.E2.80.931064-15">[14]</a></sup> complicating the design of pipelines and storage tanks.<sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Apart from its use as a reactant, <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> has wide applications in physics and engineering. It is used as a <a href="/wiki/Shielding_gas" title="Shielding gas">shielding gas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a> methods such as <a href="/wiki/Atomic_hydrogen_welding" title="Atomic hydrogen welding">atomic hydrogen welding</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup> H<sub>2</sub> is used as the rotor coolant in <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical generator">electrical generators</a> at <a href="/wiki/Power_station" title="Power station">power stations</a>, because it has the highest <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> of any gas. Liquid H<sub>2</sub> is used in <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> research, including <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductivity</a> studies.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> Because <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> is lighter than air, having a little more than <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>14</sub></span> of the density of air, it was once widely used as a <a href="/wiki/Lifting_gas" title="Lifting gas">lifting gas</a> in balloons and <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airships</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Almqvist03_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Almqvist03-104">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>In more recent applications, hydrogen is used pure or mixed with nitrogen (sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Forming_gas" title="Forming gas">forming gas</a>) as a tracer gas for minute leak detection. Applications can be found in the automotive, chemical, power generation, aerospace, and telecommunications industries.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup> Hydrogen is an authorized food additive (E 949) that allows food package leak testing among other anti-oxidizing properties.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hydrogen's rarer isotopes also each have specific applications. <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">Deuterium</a> (hydrogen-2) is used in <a href="/wiki/CANDU_reactor" title="CANDU reactor">nuclear fission applications</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">moderator</a> to slow <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> reactions.<sup id="cite_ref-nbb_8-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-8">[8]</a></sup> Deuterium compounds have applications in chemistry and biology in studies of reaction <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_isotope_effect" title="Kinetic isotope effect">isotope effects</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">Tritium</a> (hydrogen-3), produced in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>, is used in the production of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bombs</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup> as an isotopic label in the biosciences,<sup id="cite_ref-holte_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holte-59">[57]</a></sup> and as a <a href="/wiki/Beta_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Beta radiation">radiation</a> source in luminous paints.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> temperature of equilibrium hydrogen is a defining fixed point on the <a href="/wiki/International_Temperature_Scale_of_1990" title="International Temperature Scale of 1990">ITS-90</a> temperature scale at 13.8033&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coolant">Coolant</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen-cooled_turbo_generator" title="Hydrogen-cooled turbo generator">Hydrogen-cooled turbo generator</a></div>
<p>Hydrogen is commonly used in power stations as a coolant in generators due to a number of favorable properties that are a direct result of its light diatomic molecules. These include low <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>, low <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>, and the highest <a href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" class="mw-redirect" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> of all gases.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_carrier">Energy carrier</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">Hydrogen economy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_infrastructure" title="Hydrogen infrastructure">Hydrogen infrastructure</a></div>
<p>Hydrogen is not an energy resource,<sup id="cite_ref-sustain_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sustain-111">[109]</a></sup> except in the hypothetical context of commercial <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> power plants using <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a>, a technology presently far from development.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup> The Sun's energy comes from <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">nuclear fusion</a> of hydrogen, but this process is difficult to achieve controllably on Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup> Elemental hydrogen from solar, biological, or electrical sources require more energy to make it than is obtained by burning it, so in these cases hydrogen functions as an energy carrier, like a battery. Hydrogen may be obtained from fossil sources (such as methane), but these sources are unsustainable.<sup id="cite_ref-sustain_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sustain-111">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> per unit <i>volume</i> of both <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Compressed_hydrogen" title="Compressed hydrogen">compressed hydrogen</a> gas at any practicable pressure is significantly less than that of traditional fuel sources, although the energy density per unit fuel <i>mass</i> is higher.<sup id="cite_ref-sustain_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sustain-111">[109]</a></sup> Nevertheless, elemental hydrogen has been widely discussed in the context of energy, as a possible future <i>carrier</i> of energy on an economy-wide scale.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> For example, <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> <a href="/wiki/CO2_sequestration" class="mw-redirect" title="CO2 sequestration">sequestration</a> followed by <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> could be conducted at the point of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> production from fossil fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-GATech_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GATech-115">[113]</a></sup> Hydrogen used in transportation would burn relatively cleanly, with some <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NO<sub>x</sub></a> emissions,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup> but without carbon emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-GATech_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GATech-115">[113]</a></sup> However, the infrastructure costs associated with full conversion to a hydrogen economy would be substantial.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">Fuel cells</a> can convert hydrogen and oxygen directly to electricity more efficiently than internal combustion engines.<sup id="cite_ref-garbak11_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-garbak11-118">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Semiconductor_industry">Semiconductor industry</span></h3>
<p>Hydrogen is employed to saturate broken ("dangling") bonds of <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_silicon" title="Amorphous silicon">amorphous silicon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbon" title="Amorphous carbon">amorphous carbon</a> that helps stabilizing material properties.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[117]</a></sup> It is also a potential <a href="/wiki/Electron_donor" title="Electron donor">electron donor</a> in various oxide materials, including <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">ZnO</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tin_dioxide" title="Tin dioxide">SnO<sub>2</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_oxide" title="Cadmium oxide">CdO</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_oxide" title="Magnesium oxide">MgO</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zirconium_dioxide" title="Zirconium dioxide">ZrO<sub>2</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Hafnium(IV)_oxide" title="Hafnium(IV) oxide">HfO<sub>2</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum(III)_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Lanthanum(III) oxide">La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Yttrium(III)_oxide" title="Yttrium(III) oxide">Y<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide">TiO<sub>2</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Strontium_titanate" title="Strontium titanate">SrTiO<sub>3</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum_aluminate" title="Lanthanum aluminate">LaAlO<sub>3</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">SiO<sub>2</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Zircon" title="Zircon">ZrSiO<sub>4</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Hafnon" title="Hafnon">HfSiO<sub>4</sub></a>, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Strontium_zirconate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Strontium zirconate (page does not exist)">SrZrO<sub>3</sub></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_reactions">Biological reactions</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biohydrogen" title="Biohydrogen">Biohydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biological_hydrogen_production_(Algae)" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological hydrogen production (Algae)">Biological hydrogen production (Algae)</a></div>
<p>H<sub>2</sub> is a product of some types of <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">anaerobic metabolism</a> and is produced by several <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>, usually via reactions <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyzed</a> by <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>- or <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>-containing <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> called <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenase" title="Hydrogenase">hydrogenases</a>. These enzymes catalyze the reversible <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> reaction between H<sub>2</sub> and its component two protons and two electrons. Creation of hydrogen gas occurs in the transfer of reducing equivalents produced during <a href="/wiki/Pyruvate" class="mw-redirect" title="Pyruvate">pyruvate</a> <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> to water.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup> The natural cycle of hydrogen production and consumption by organisms is called the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cycle" title="Hydrogen cycle">hydrogen cycle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rhee6_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rhee6-125">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Water_splitting" title="Water splitting">Water splitting</a>, in which water is decomposed into its component protons, electrons, and oxygen, occurs in the <a href="/wiki/Light-dependent_reactions" title="Light-dependent reactions">light reactions</a> in all <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Photosynthetic">photosynthetic</a> organisms. Some such organisms, including the alga <i><a href="/wiki/Chlamydomonas_reinhardtii" title="Chlamydomonas reinhardtii">Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, have evolved a second step in the <a href="/wiki/Dark_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Dark reaction">dark reactions</a> in which protons and electrons are reduced to form H<sub>2</sub> gas by specialized hydrogenases in the <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplast</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup> Efforts have been undertaken to genetically modify cyanobacterial hydrogenases to efficiently synthesize H<sub>2</sub> gas even in the presence of oxygen.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup> Efforts have also been undertaken with genetically modified <a href="/wiki/Biological_hydrogen_production_(Algae)" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological hydrogen production (Algae)">alga in a bioreactor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_and_precautions">Safety and precautions</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_safety" title="Hydrogen safety">Hydrogen safety</a></div>
<p>Hydrogen poses a number of hazards to human safety, from potential <a href="/wiki/Detonation" title="Detonation">detonations</a> and fires when mixed with air to being an <a href="/wiki/Asphyxia" title="Asphyxia">asphyxiant</a> in its pure, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>-free form.<sup id="cite_ref-NASAH2_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASAH2-129">[127]</a></sup> In addition, liquid hydrogen is a <a href="/wiki/Cryogen" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryogen">cryogen</a> and presents dangers (such as <a href="/wiki/Frostbite" title="Frostbite">frostbite</a>) associated with very cold liquids.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup> Hydrogen dissolves in many metals, and, in addition to leaking out, may have adverse effects on them, such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_embrittlement" title="Hydrogen embrittlement">hydrogen embrittlement</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup> leading to cracks and explosions.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup> Hydrogen gas leaking into external air may spontaneously ignite. Moreover, hydrogen fire, while being extremely hot, is almost invisible, and thus can lead to accidental burns.<sup id="cite_ref-Cunn88_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cunn88-133">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Even interpreting the hydrogen data (including safety data) is confounded by a number of phenomena. Many physical and chemical properties of hydrogen depend on the <a href="/wiki/Spin_isomers_of_hydrogen" title="Spin isomers of hydrogen">parahydrogen/orthohydrogen</a> ratio (it often takes days or weeks at a given temperature to reach the equilibrium ratio, for which the data is usually given). Hydrogen detonation parameters, such as critical detonation pressure and temperature, strongly depend on the container geometry.<sup id="cite_ref-NASAH2_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASAH2-129">[127]</a></sup></p>
